%  / Tm
%  k
%  / grkv (T )m
%  / 0
% \bullet ,\bullet Definition 9.3. The spectral sequence of the double complex M (with respect to
% the vertical filtration) is the spectral sequence determined by this exact
% couple. ?
% I will denote this spectral sequence by v E, with the absurdly positioned index
% v
% recording that v E arises from the vertical filtration. The horizontal
% filtration leads
% likewise to a spectral sequence h E.
% All terms v E r of the spectral sequence are subquotients of the first one, v E
% 1 =
% H \bullet  (gr\bullet  v (T )).
% Remark 9.4. The differential dr = \beta r \circ  \gamma r acts by increasing the
% cohomological
% degree k by 1 and the filtration degree m by r: indeed, \gamma r is simply
% induced by \gamma ,
% so it increases both k and m by 1 (as observed above); \beta r is obtained by
% applying \beta
% after undoing (r - 1) times the effect of \alpha  (as pointed out after Claim
% 9.1), so it
% does not change k but increases m by (r - 1).
%  ?
% Interest in the sequence rests on the interpretation for its limit in the
% statement
% that follows, which also summarizes the situation.
% \bullet ,\bullet Theorem 9.5. Let M be a double complex in an abelian category. Assume that
% i,j \bullet  \bullet ,\bullet M = 0 for i < 0, j < 0, and let T be the total
% complex of M . Then, with
% notation as above, there exists a spectral sequence {( v E i , di )}i such that
% v E 1 \sim 
%  = H
%  \bullet 
%  (grv \bullet  (T )),
%  v E \infty  = \sim 
%  grv H
%  \bullet 
%  (T \bullet ).
% In other words, `turning the crank' moves the gr from inside the cohomology
% to outside of it. The limit v E \infty  does not quite compute the cohomology of
% the
% total complex, as I glibly announced in \S{}8.3, but it computes the graded
% object
% determined by a filtration on the cohomology of the total complex, and this is
% good
% enough for many applications.
% Proof. The limit v E \infty  can be computed directly from the exact couple
% (Exer-
% cise 9.8), as a subquotient of v E 1 : it is the quotient Z\infty  /B\infty  ,
% where
% ?
%  \setminus 
% Z\infty  = \gamma  -1 ( im \alpha r ), B\infty  = \beta ( ker \alpha r ).
% r
%  r
% The computation is streamlined by focusing on a specific degree k for the
% cohomol-
% k \bullet 
%  \bullet 
% ogy and m for the grading. The corresponding part in v E 1 is H (Tm
% /Tm+1
%  ), and
% \alpha  acts as H k
%  (Tm) \bullet 
%  \to  H k
%  (Tm-1 \bullet 
%  ). Note that
% if r ? 0, then H k (Tm+r
%  \bullet 
%  ) = 0,
%  H k (Tm-r
%  \bullet 
%  ) = H k (T \bullet  ),
% under our boundedness hypothesis: indeed, Ti j = 0 if i > j, and Ti \bullet  = T
% \bullet  for i \leq  0.
% By the first of these ?observations, r
%  the piece sent by \alpha r to H k (Tm
%  \bullet 
%  ) is 0 for r ? 0,
% and it follows that im \alpha  = 0. Therefore,
% rZ\infty  = ker \gamma  = im \beta .
% By the second observation, ker \alpha r stabilizes to ker(H k (Tm
%  \bullet 
%  ) \to  H k (T \bullet  )) for each k,
% hence
%  !
% B\infty  = \beta (
%  ker(H \bullet  (Tm
% ) \bullet 
%  \to  H \bullet  (T \bullet  ))).
% m
% Denote by \nu m : H \bullet  (Tm
% ) \bullet 
%  \to  H \bullet  (T \bullet ) the morphisms induced in cohomology by the
% "monomorphisms Tm \bullet 
%  \to  T \bullet  and by \nu  =
%  \nu m their direct sum. We have morphisms
% \beta 
%  "
%  \nu 
%  "H \bullet  (gr\bullet  v (T )) o
%  m H
%  \bullet 
%  (Tm
% )
%  \bullet 
%  / m
%  H \bullet  (T \bullet  ) ,
% and we have obtained
% v E \infty  \sim 
%  =
%  im \beta 
%  .
% \beta (ker \nu )
% By the innocent Exercise 2.12,
% im \beta  \sim  =
%  im \nu 
%  ;
% \beta (ker \nu )
%  \nu (ker \beta )
% by the exactness of the original couple,
% !
% ker \beta  = im \alpha  =
%  im(H \bullet (Tm+1
%  \bullet 
%  ) \to  H \bullet  (Tm
%  \bullet 
%  )),
% m
% hence
%  !
% \nu (ker \beta ) =
%  im \nu m+1 .
% m
% Therefore
% ! im \nu m
%  \bullet  \bullet v E \infty  \sim 
%  =
%  = grv (H (T ))
% im
%  \nu 
% m+1
% m
% as stated.
%  ?
% Theorem 9.5 is a substantial generalization of Theorems 8.9 and 8.12. To see
% that it implies Theorem 8.12, note again that
% ! Tm k
%  !
% grkv (T ) =
%  k
%  \sim 
%  =
%  M m,k-m,
% m
%  Tm+1
%  m
% with its `vertical' differential \delta v . The term v E 1 of the spectral
% sequence is the
% cohomology of this complex, that is, the cohomology of the columns of the orig-
% inal double complex, suitably shifted. If the cohomology of each column M
% i,\bullet  is
% concentrated in degree 0, then v E 1 is simply the complex
% (*)
%  \cdot \cdot \cdot 
%  / 0
%  / H 0 (M 0,\bullet  )
%  / H 0 (M 1,\bullet  )
%  / H 0 (M 2,\bullet )
%  / \cdot \cdot \cdot  ;
% hence v E 2 is the cohomology of this complex, and higher differentials of the
% spec-
% tral sequence are 0 by degree considerations. It follows that the sequence
% collapses
% \bullet  \bullet at v E 2 , and by Theorem 9.5 this says that grv (H (T )) is isomorphic to the coho-
% mology of (*). In this case we clearly have grv (H \bullet  (T \bullet )) = \sim
%  H \bullet  (T \bullet  ), so the conclusion
% reproduces the corresponding statement in Theorem 8.12. This is what is meant by
% the incantation `by an immediate spectral sequence argument' I mentioned in the
% beginning of this subsection.
% This reasoning and other applications of the spectral sequence of a double
% complex are easier to understand if the various degrees and shifts are
% visualized in
% a more compelling way. View the original double complex
% ..
%  ..
%  ..
%  ..
% . O
%  . O
%  . O
%  . O
% M 0,2
%  O
%  M 1,2
%  O
%  M 2,2
%  O
%  M 3,2
%  O
%  \cdot \cdot \cdot 
% M 0,1
%  O
%  M 1,1
%  O
%  M 2,1
%  O
%  M 3,1
%  O
%  \cdot \cdot \cdot 
% M 0,0
%  M 1,0
%  M 2,0
%  M 3,0
%  \cdot \cdot \cdot 
% as an `E0' term in the sequence. The horizontal differentials are dotted out to
% highlight the fact that v E 1 is obtained by taking the cohomology of the
% columns;
% this gives
% H 2 (T0 \bullet  /T1 \bullet  )
%  / H 3 (T1 \bullet  /T2 \bullet )
%  / H 4 (T2 \bullet  /T3 \bullet )
%  / H 5(T3 \bullet  /T4 \bullet  )
%  / \cdot \cdot \cdot 
% H 1 (T0 \bullet  /T1 \bullet  )
%  / H 2 (T1 \bullet  /T2 \bullet )
%  / H 3 (T2 \bullet  /T3 \bullet )
%  / H 4(T3 \bullet  /T4 \bullet  )
%  / \cdot \cdot \cdot 
% H 0 (T0 \bullet  /T1 \bullet  )
%  / H 1 (T1 \bullet  /T2 \bullet )
%  / H 2 (T2 \bullet  /T3 \bullet )
%  / H 3(T3 \bullet  /T4 \bullet  )
%  / \cdot \cdot \cdot 
% For example, the part in degree 2 of H 3 (gr\bullet  v (T )) is the cohomology
% of the second
% column M 2,\bullet  computed at gr3 v (T ) 2 = M 2,1 , so I placed the
% corresponding object
% 3 \bullet  \bullet H (T2 /T3 ) in position (2, 1). The differential of v E 1
% increases both the cohomolog-
% ical degree and the filtration degree by 1, as indicated. Such diagrams are
% somewhat
% hard to parse. To simplify dealing with the indices, it is common to assign
% degrees
% (i, j) to the terms in v E 1 according to the indices of the objects M i,j = M
% m,k-m
% at which the cohomology is computed; that is, set
% v E i,j
%  1 = H
%  i+j
%  (Ti \bullet /Ti+1
%  \bullet 
%  ).
% Then the same array is drawn as
% v E 1
%  0,2
%  / v E 1,2
%  / v E 1
%  2,2
%  / v E 3,2
%  / \cdot \cdot \cdot 
% v E 1
%  0,1
%  / v E 1
%  1,1
%  / v E 2,1
%  / v E 3,1
%  / \cdot \cdot \cdot 
% v E 1
%  0,0
%  / v E 1
%  1,0
%  / v E 2,0
%  / v E 3,0
%  / \cdot \cdot \cdot 
% The next term v E 2 is obtained by taking the cohomology of v E 1 . Its
% differential
% acts by increasing m by 2 and k by 1, that is, i by 2 and j by -1. We can draw
% this as
% v E 0,2
%  0,1
%  2 TT
%  TT Tv T TT E 1,2
%  2 1,1
%  T T
%  T T TT T TT T
% T T *
%  v T E TT 2,2
%  2,1
%  2 TT
%  TT T TT T T
%  TT T *
%  T v E TT 2
%  3,2
%  3,1
%  T
% T TT T
% T T)
%  \cdot \cdot \cdot 
% v E 0,0
%  2 TT
%  TT Tv T TT E
%  1,0
%  2 T T
%  T T TT T TT T
% T T *
%  v T TT E
%  2,0
%  2 TT
%  TT T TT T T
%  TT T *
%  v T E
%  TT 3,0
%  T
% T TT T
% T T)
%  \cdot \cdot \cdot 
% v E 2
%  v E 2
%  v E 2
%  v E 2
%  \cdot \cdot \cdot 
% In general, v E r is the cohomology of v E r-1, and its differential increases m
% by r
% and k by 1 (Remark 9.4); that is, (i, j) ?\to  (i+r, j -(r -1)). Pictorially,
% differentials
% at different stages act like this:
% v E1
%  v E2
%  v E3
% and so on. This picture of a `rotating' differential may be the image that most
% people
% have in mind when they think of a spectral sequence. The argument sketched
% above, deriving Theorem 8.12 from Theorem 9.5, is probably clearer from this
% point of view: if the cohomology of the columns is concentrated in degree 0,
% then
% v E 1 is concentrated along the bottom row of the array. It is then clear that
% all
% differentials di for i \geq  2 will have to be zero, since either their source
% or their target
% is zero. Thus the sequence collapses at v E 2 and yields the cohomology of the
% total
% complex by Theorem 9.5. The other situation considered in Theorem 8.12 can be
% analyzed by using the sequence hE i obtained from the horizontal filtration.

\subsection*{Exercises}

% Of course, spectral sequences are not limited to these simple applications. The
% Grothendieck spectral sequence `computes' the derived functor of the composition
% of two functors42 : for example, if F : A \to  B and G : B \to  C are two
% right-exact
% functors and F sends projectives to projectives, then there is a spectral
% sequence
% whose E2 term collects the compositions Lp G \circ  Lq F and that abuts to Lp+q
% (G \circ  F ).
% For instance, if f : R \to  S is a homomorphism of commutative rings (so that
% S may be viewed as an R-module), A is an R-module, and B is an S-module
% (and hence an R-module, via f ), then there is a `change-of-ring spectral
% sequence'
% TorSp (TorR
%  q (A, S), B) \Rightarrow  Tor\bullet  R
%  (A, B). In topology, the Serre spectral sequence can
% be used to compute the homology of a fibration in terms of the homology of the
% base
% and of the fiber. It would be completely futile for me to attempt to do any
% justice to
% the range of applications of spectral sequences: the set of mathematicians X
% such
% that there is an important X-spectral sequence includes (but is not limited to)
% Adams, Atiyah, Barratt, Bloch, Bockstein, Bousfield, Cartan, Connes, Eilenberg,
% Federer, Fr\"olicher, Green, Grothendieck, Hirzebruch, Hochschild, Hodge,
% Hurewicz,
% van Kampen, Kan, Kunneth, Leray, Lichtenbaum, Lyndon, May, Miller, Milnor,
% Moore, Novikov, de Rham, Quillen, Rothenberg, Serre, Steenrod, . . . .
% Many important spectral sequences may be derived as particular instances of
% the Grothendieck spectral sequence mentioned above. The reader will have the
% pleasure of seeing how this spectral sequence is put together by working out
% Exer-
% cise 9.10.
% 9.1. Let A be an abelian category. Since the objects of K(A) and D(A) are sim-
% ply cochain complexes, a `universal Euler characteristic' \chi  is defined for
% bounded
% complexes in these categories (see Exercise 3.15). Prove that if
% A
%  E 4 4
% +1
%  4 4
% ?
% C o
%  B
% is a distinguished triangle in K(A) or D(A), then \chi (B) = \chi (A) + \chi
% (C).
% 9.2. ? Let A be an abelian category, and suppose two complexes L\bullet , M
% \bullet  are
% connected by an `upside-down roof' (a `trough' ?)
% L\bullet >
%  > >
%  > >
%  ?
%  ~ ~ ~~
%  ~
%  M quasi-isomorphism
%  \bullet 
% \bullet 
% N42For a different viewpoint on this question, see Remark 7.4.
% \bullet 
% Prove that they are also connected by a regular roof: there exists a complex
% Nand morphisms N \bullet  \to  L\bullet  and N \bullet  \to  M \bullet  such
% that the diagram
% \bullet quasi-isomorphism ?~~
%  ~
%  ~~
%  N B
%  B B
%  B B
% L\bullet >
%  > >
%  > >
%  ?
%  ~ ~ ~
%  ~
%  ~
%  M quasi-isomorphism
%  \bullet 
% \bullet 
% N
% commutes in K(A). Deduce that the composition of two roofs may be consolidated
% into a single roof in K(A). (Hint: Let \alpha \bullet  : L\bullet  \to  N
% \bullet , \beta  : M \bullet  \to  N \bullet  be the given
% morphisms, with \beta  a quasi-isomorphism. Consider the composition \gamma
% \bullet  : L\bullet  \to
% N \bullet  \to  M C(\beta )\bullet , and let N \bullet  := M C(\gamma
% )[-1]\bullet  . We have N i = Li \oplus  M i \oplus  N i-1 , with
% morphisms to Li , M i given, respectively, by ( , m, n) ?\to  , ( , m, n) ?\to
% -m; these
% define morphisms of cochain complexes. The morphisms N i \to  N i-1 defined by
% ( , m, n) ?\to  -n define the homotopy showing that the diagram commutes in
% K(A).
% To verify that N \bullet  \to  L\bullet  is a quasi-isomorphism, use the fact
% that M C(\beta )\bullet  is exact,
% and rotate a triangle.) [\S{}9.1]
% 9.3. ? Let \alpha \bullet  : L\bullet  \to  M \bullet  be an isomorphism of
% cochain complexes. Prove that
% M C(\alpha )\bullet  is homotopy equivalent to 0. [\S{}9.2, 9.4]
% 9.4. ? Let \alpha \bullet  : L\bullet  \to  M \bullet  be a cochain morphism,
% and let \beta  \bullet  : M \bullet  \to  M C(\alpha )\bullet  be
% the natural morphism. Prove that M C(\beta )\bullet  is homotopy equivalent to
% L[1]\bullet  . (Hint:
% Do Exercise 9.3 first.) [\S{}9.2]
% 9.5. ? Let
% \alpha \bullet 
%  \bullet  \beta  \bullet 
%  \bullet 
%  / L\bullet  / M / N / 0
% be a short exact sequence of cochain complexes on an abelian category.
% \bullet  Prove that there is a cochain morphism \gamma  \bullet  : M C(\alpha )\bullet  \to  N \bullet  through which
% \beta  \bullet  : M \bullet  \to  N \bullet  factors.
% \bullet  Prove that M C(\gamma )\bullet  is an exact complex. (Hint: Chase elements.)
% \bullet  Conclude that M C(\alpha )\bullet  is quasi-isomorphic to N \bullet  .
% [\S{}9.2]
% 9.6. ? Let
%  / L\bullet  \alpha \bullet 
%  / M \bullet  \beta  \bullet 
%  N \bullet 
%  / 0
% /be a short exact sequence of cochain complexes on an abelian category A. Prove
% that there is a morphism N \bullet  \to  L[1]\bullet  in the derived category
% D(A), inducing the
% connecting morphism H i (N \bullet  ) \to  H i+1 (L\bullet  ) in cohomology.
% [\S{}9.2]
% 9.7. ? Prove Claim 9.1. [\S{}9.3]
% 9.8. ? Let (Er , dr )r=1,2,... be a spectral sequence arising from an exact
% couple
% A
%  E 3 3
% \gamma 
%  3 3\alpha 
% 3 3
% ?
% E o
%  A
% \beta 
% ?
%  ?
%  rProve that the limit E\infty  is isomorphic to \gamma  -1 ( r im \alpha r
%  )/\beta ( r ker \alpha  ). (Hint: In
% fact, Zr+1 = \gamma -1 (im \alpha r ) and Br+1 = \beta (ker \alpha r ).)
% [\S{}9.3]
% 9.9. Let v E r be the spectral sequence of a first-quadrant double complex from
% the
% vertical filtration, as in \S{}9.3. Prove that v E i,j
%  r = v E \infty  i,j
%  for r > j + 1.
% 9.10. ? Let A, B, C be abelian categories, and let F : A \to  B, G : B \to  C be
% additive functors. Assume that A and B have enough projectives, that F sends
% projectives of A to G -acyclic objects of B, and that G is right-exact.
% Let A be an object of A, and let M \bullet  : \dots  \to  M -2 \to  M -1 \to  M
% 0 \to  0 be
% \bullet a projective resolution of A. Let P M \bullet  be a Cartan-Eilenberg resolution of the
% Fcomplex F M \bullet  (as constructed in Corollary 7.9). Consider the complex
% (\dagger )
%  \cdot \cdot \cdot 
%  / G (PF \bullet

% obtained by applying G to PF \bullet  M \bullet  .
% This is precisely the set-up of Exercise 8.8. Now take the double complex asso-
% ciated with (\dagger ), and construct the spectral sequence corresponding to the
% horizontal
% filtration. Prove that the E2 term of this sequence has terms
% h E p,q
%  2 = Lp G (Lq F (A)).
% (Use Exercise 7.6 to compute the hE 1 term.)
% This is the Grothendieck spectral sequence mentioned at the end of \S{}9.3.
% Prove
% that it abuts to L\bullet  (G \circ  F )(A).
